sleeping well, not gaining unneeded weight, and striving to
maintain a body that can hold up to the amount of running I want
to do.
That last point is where the goal really helps me. It means that
pretty much every day I do strengthening, core stability, balance,
or mobility exercises. Some I do immediately before and after
running, and others I do throughout the day. I still have my
trouble spots; I still wish I were a little more flowing when
running, and I still sometimes miss days to injury. (I don’t run if I
sense that I can’t do so with my normal gait—running in that
compromised state will both exacerbate the current issue and set
me up for compensatory problems elsewhere.) But as someone
who has already run more than 130,000 miles, committing to
doing nonrunning exercises daily has been the key to consistent,
enjoyable running for the last few decades.
Another practice I credit with meeting the miss-no-days goal is
having lots of variety in my running. That’s variety in terms of
distance, intensity, terrain, and topography. Most of my weeks
look a lot like a sample week from the training schedules later in
this book. I regularly mix long runs, short recovery days, tempo
runs, interval workouts, turnover sessions, and general aerobic
runs. It’s when I get away from multipace running—when I string
together too many medium-length, medium-intensity days—that I
start to feel flat, stiff, and achy. Thanks to all of the above, I’ve
had only three injuries requiring a week or more off from running
since I turned 40.
A bonus: When I do decide to focus on a competitive goal, the
default setting of variety sets me up well to quickly pivot to more
structured training. (Well, quickly for someone who has been
running more than 45 years.) I can be in decent road 5K shape
in a handful of weeks, or I can reasonably target a long trail race
two months hence. It will be from this baseline of well-rounded
--- PAGE 194 ---
fitness that I’ll build to meet a goal of running a road marathon in
my teens, 20s, and every decade on up to my 60s.
It doesn’t happen as often as it did 30 years ago, and certainly
not as often as I’d like, but at least a few times a month I have
what I call a “yes” run. I’ll realize I’m floating along, unburdened
by any physical or mental obstacles to being completely
absorbed in the moment. “Yes,” I think, and nothing more. I
usually have to force myself to start heading home. If putting a
little effort into bodily maintenance means I can regularly have
such runs, I’ll happily take that trade-off.
—Scott Douglas
Common sense involves not only listening to your body’s signals but
also accepting that the best way to heed those signals could be different
from when you were younger. In your 20s you could probably run through
sudden small aches and pains. Now it’s more likely that the same approach
will lead to the issue being noticeable before, during, and after running, and
you’ll realize your training has been compromised for the last few weeks.
Older runners simply have a smaller margin of error in finessing their way
through these situations.
It’s almost always best to err on the side of caution. If you feel an injury
coming on—especially one that gets worse the longer you run—don’t
delude yourself. Attempting to run through it will usually make the injury
worse; instead of one or two days off now, you might find yourself having
to miss weeks to recover. Cross-train until you feel you can run normally
and, if required, get some treatment.
Similarly, if you feel a chest cold coming on, don’t push through a
challenging run in the rain or snow. Instead, go home and get warm and,
hopefully, ward off a chest infection that might compromise your training
for several weeks.
Common sense also includes subtle modifications to your running
routine. The days of rolling out of bed and straight into an early-morning
run are over. (Not that doing so was ever a good idea, but the consequences
are likely to be much greater now.) If, as is common, your schedule requires
fitting in marathon training before work, include prerun prep time when
--- PAGE 195 ---
deciding when to wake. Use the dynamic flexibility warm-up described in
chapter 4 to ensure that you start your workout with good running form;
stiffly stumbling around the first few miles increases your risk of injury. In
addition, allow adequate time for a comprehensive warm-up before hard
workouts.
Common sense and discipline overlap in that the latter often means
making what you know are the right choices, even though in the moment
those choices aren’t as fun and carefree as you might wish they were.
Discipline for older marathoners also involves being dedicated about
maintaining your running body through regular flexibility, core stability,
and strength training work. If you have a more personalized program from a
physical therapist or other sports medicine professional, discipline entails
sticking to it even when the acute issue that led to seeing the professional
seems to have improved. As coach, author, and U.S. masters record-holder
Pete Magill puts it, if you think you don’t have the time for this ongoing
maintenance work, you will soon when you’re injured and can’t run.
Being a disciplined older runner isn’t just about avoiding injury
(although that should be a powerful enough inducement). Four-time
Olympian Meb Keflezighi maintained a high level of performance into his
40s by stretching before and after every run, doing core stability and
strengthening work almost daily, and performing running form drills six
days a week. Keflezighi also replaced some of his shorter recovery runs
with ElliptiGO rides so that he was better recovered for his key workouts.
You may not have as much time as Keflezighi did to focus on the little
things that help you run strong and injury-free, but within your personal
context you can show a similar level of discipline.
OceanofPDF.com
--- PAGE 196 ---
T
6
TAPERING FOR PEAK
MARATHON PERFORMANCE
raining provides the long-term improvements in fitness that are
necessary for marathon success. As you’re no doubt aware, training
also tends to leave you a bit tired most of the time. As we’ve noted,
this doesn’t mean you’re overtrained—a moderate amount of residual
--- PAGE 197 ---
fatigue is fine during the many weeks of training. The periodic recovery
weeks in your training schedule are designed to reduce but not totally
eliminate the accumulated fatigue of training.
When the marathon approaches, however, you need to cut back your
training for a more prolonged period so you’re optimally rested. Tapering
your training is critical for reaching the starting line in peak fitness and with
maximal energy reserves. The challenge during the last several weeks
leading up to the marathon is to find the best balance between continued
training (to be in the best possible racing shape) and resting (to eliminate
fatigue). In this chapter, we’ll look at the best way to strike that crucial
balance.
BENEFITS OF TAPERING
Put simply, tapering corrects the accumulated wear and tear of training.
More specifically, it appears that tapering leads to improvements in running
economy (how much oxygen you need to run at a given pace), muscle
strength, and power. As we saw in chapter 1, improvements in running
economy relate directly to improvements in marathon race pace, so tapering
is money in the bank in improving your marathon performance. Renowned
exercise physiologist Tim Noakes, MD, emphasizes that recovery of the
shock-absorbing function of the muscles is an important benefit of tapering
for marathon runners, which may explain the improvements in running
economy (Noakes 2003). Tapering allows repair of the ongoing
microcellular muscle damage from training, fully replenishes the glycogen
stores in your muscles and liver, and bolsters your immune system. Other
indicators of recovery from stress during tapering include decreases in
mood disturbance, positive changes in hormonal markers of stress, and
improved sleep (Bosquet et al. 2007; Mujika 2010). How much benefit you
gain from a taper likely depends on how much and for how long you have
ramped up your marathon training, as well as your individual capacity to
adapt and recover.
Scientific studies on tapering for distance runners typically find
performance improvements of 2 to 3 percent, which equates to about 3.5 to
--- PAGE 198 ---
5.5 minutes for a 3-hour marathon (Bosquet et al. 2007; Grivas 2018). The
potential gains from tapering are substantial.
HOW LONG SHOULD YOU TAPER?
Several studies investigating the relationship between racing performance at
various distances and taper duration concluded that the optimal length of a
taper is seven days to three weeks. For the marathon, the general consensus
is to taper for a minimum of two weeks, with three weeks being optimal.
Too short a taper will leave you tired on marathon day, whereas tapering for
too long will lead to a loss of fitness. When you consider that any one
workout will improve fitness by perhaps 1 percent, while a well-designed
taper can provide an improvement in race performance of several
percentage points, it’s wise to err on the side of tapering too much rather
than not enough. For the marathon, a well-designed three-week taper will
leave you optimally prepared and recovered for the race.
KEY PRINCIPLES FOR MARATHON
TAPERING
Begin tapering three weeks before your marathon.
Maintain training intensity.
Reduce mileage.
Make recovery days easy or take days off.
Optimize recovery strategies with proper diet and hydration.
Eliminate muscle tightness with stretching, recovery tools
(such as foam rollers), physical therapy (if required),
massage, and rest.
HOW SHOULD YOU REDUCE YOUR
TRAINING TO IMPROVE RACING
--- PAGE 199 ---
PERFORMANCE?
Both scientific and anecdotal evidence indicates that the key to effective
tapering is to substantially reduce your mileage while maintaining the
intensity of your training (Mujika 2010; Grivas 2018). Reducing the amount
you run reduces accumulated fatigue to improve your marathon
performance, and interspersing efforts throughout your buildup, including
V O2max intervals and strides, maintains the adaptations you have worked
hard to gain over the past several months. During your taper, you should do
shorter sessions at those efforts, so while you’re maintaining the intensity of
training, you’re still reducing the quantity of higher-intensity training. We
also recommend a tune-up race two weeks before the marathon as a key
session for putting the final polish on your racing fitness (and, ideally,
providing psychological reinforcement that you’re almost ready to roll).
How much you reduce your overall mileage depends on your current
training volume, past experience (know thyself), and overall health. In
general, older runners tend to require slightly more time to taper than
younger runners. Based on research, many discussions with elite
marathoners and coaches, and personal experience, we recommend the
following for cutting back your mileage:
Third week before the marathon: Reduce mileage by 20 to 30
percent.
Second week before the marathon: Reduce mileage by 40 percent.
Marathon week (six days before race): Reduce mileage by 65
percent.
For example, a marathoner whose training peaks at 70 miles (113 km)
per week would reduce mileage by about 20 to 30 percent (to 49 to 56 miles
[79 to 90 km]) in the third week before the race, by about 40 percent (to 42
miles [68 km]) the following week, and by about 65 percent (to 28 miles
[45 km]) during race week. For race week, the 65 percent mileage reduction
is for the six days leading up to the marathon.
Emily Sisson
--- PAGE 200 ---
Fastest marathon: 2:18:29 (2022 Chicago Marathon)
Marathon highlights: second in the 2022 Chicago Marathon,
second in the 2024 U.S. Olympic Trials
Emily Sisson is a refreshing reminder that the bulk of
marathoning success comes from mastering the basics rather
than obsessing over “hacks” and “secrets.”
Description
Take, for example, Sisson’s setup. After finishing her
collegiate career in 2015 with two NCAA titles to her name,
Sisson could have joined any high-profile training group she
wanted. Instead, she stayed in Providence, Rhode Island, to
continue being coached by Providence College coach Ray
Treacy. While Providence was an elite magnet in the 1980s,
most pros now seek less-urban locales. But Sisson knew that
the chance to keep working with Treacy and train with
Olympians Molly Huddle and Kim Smith trumped a new training
system in a new setting. Her payoffs have included making two
Olympic teams and setting U.S. records in the half marathon
and marathon.
--- PAGE 201 ---
About that training: Treacy’s system is grounded in multipace
training. Sisson regularly touches on a wide range of
intensities: 5K race pace and faster, tempo runs at around half
marathon effort, and long runs that incorporate several miles at
marathon race pace or faster. Again, nothing fancy. Perhaps
even simple. But as jazz legend Thelonious Monk put it, simple
ain’t easy. Committing to proven principles and consistently
doing that unflashy work almost always serves runners better
than frequently switching from one training trend to the next.
Not that Sisson is uninterested in innovation. She’s up to date
on best practices such as rehydrating and refueling soon after
tiring runs to speed recovery. And with the help of her husband,
Shane Quinn, who is a therapist, she’s a leading proponent of
using sport psychology to help her mind get the most out of her
body. Quinn often paces Sisson via bike on hard workouts. If
she worries that she’s not hitting the time goals of the session,
Quinn helps her to reframe her performance by reminding her
of the workout’s purpose in the big picture—a few seconds here
and there don’t matter much. This reminder helps her relax; in
turn, she often winds up running the prescribed pace.
Quinn also helps Sisson prepare mentally for races. In lieu of
extensive visualization, however, they’ve learned that for
Sisson, simpler is better. Her version of chunking—breaking the
marathon distance into manageable segments—takes the form
of setting goals for a few mile marks en route. For example,
“Aim to get to 10 miles no faster than [amount of time] while
feeling relaxed.” Sisson then spends the stretch leading up to
that signpost meeting the goal as efficiently as possible.
Sisson’s strong mental game had an unusual aspect when
she set the American record—she finished the race not
knowing her time or place. Sisson ran behind two male pacers
who were told to run 5:18 per mile, with slight adjustments for
wind direction. That pace would have resulted in breaking the
previous record by about 15 seconds. Coach Treacy told
Sisson to lock in behind the pacers and to speed up only in the
final 5K. She followed the instructions to perfection, never
--- PAGE 202 ---
looking at her watch, running her fastest 5K between 35K and
40K, and breaking the previous mark by 43 seconds. This is a
valuable strategy to keep in mind if your marathon offers pace
groups for your goal finish time: Trust that the pace group
leader will set out at the proper speed. (Check a few milestones
in the first half to be sure.) Settle into that rhythm through at
least 18 or 20 miles (30 or 32 km). If you’re still feeling frisky
after that point, strike out on your own.
Sisson’s greatest strength is perhaps her self-knowledge.
She started her college career at the University of Wisconsin
but transferred to Providence after her freshman year—she
recognized Wisconsin wasn’t a good fit for her. She set her
U.S. marathon record in a low-profile shoe more akin to the
racing flats of decades ago than this decade’s ubiquitous super
shoes. That footwear choice came after trying her sponsor’s
offerings and deciding that the shoes she felt most comfortable
in were the right ones for racing.
Sisson has gravitated toward flat, fast marathons—she
debuted in London and set her U.S. record in Chicago—in part
because those are the courses where she knows she can be
most competitive. Alternatively, you might discover that you do
best on more undulating courses. We know runners who have
fared better on courses like Boston or New York City than
Chicago or Berlin. The important thing is to learn what works for
you and not change things just for the sake of novelty. If this
approach is good enough for an Olympian and U.S. record-
holder, it’s probably good enough for the rest of us.
Three weeks before your marathon is arguably the most important time
for a successful taper. This is the week that many marathoners do too much,
because race day still seems a long way off. If you work too hard during
this week, you may find yourself feeling flat with two weeks to go and
struggling to rest up as quickly as possible for the race. It’s much better
physiologically and psychologically to allow your body to start to freshen
up during this week. This will put you in a more relaxed state of mind,
--- PAGE 203 ---
feeling that your marathon preparation is on track rather than stressing that
all of your efforts are going to be wasted.
Marathoners tend to progressively decrease their training efforts during
their marathon taper. This method presents two problems. First, by steadily
decreasing training effort over three weeks, they risk a small loss in fitness
(adaptations to training are lost at about the same rate that they are gained)
before the marathon.
Another problem is psychological. The steady-reduction approach
doesn’t provide any psychological reinforcement. Marathoners generally
need reminders that they’re still fit or their confidence may become shaky.
The more effective approach to tapering is to intersperse harder efforts
within an overall trend of recovery. Figure 6.1 shows a three-week
marathon taper in which harder efforts are included every few days. This
type of taper will leave you fit, rested, and confident for the marathon.
Figure 6.1 Marathon tapering with the right balance of hard efforts and recovery.
Description
Leading up to the marathon, it is paramount to believe in your training
program by adjusting your taper to fit your individual needs. Keep a record
of your taper leading up to each of your marathons and how you felt and
performed in those races. Over time, you’ll be able to identify patterns that
will allow you to fine-tune your taper and gain confidence that your taper
has given you the maximal edge in performance. You can then apply those
--- PAGE 204 ---
insights to customize the tapering portion of your training schedule. For
example, many runners prefer a day off quite soon before the marathon. If
you’ve found that works for you, we recommend taking that complete rest
day two or three days before the marathon. It’s almost always best to do a
light jog the day before the marathon—you’ll feel better on race day, you’ll
have a chance to get a last check on any tight muscles, and perhaps most
important, you’ll do something other than stare at the walls all day fretting
about your race.
Based on the optimal tapering criteria we’ve just discussed, table 6.1
presents a sample tapering schedule for a marathon. This tapering program
is taken from the 18-week training schedule in chapter 10. By following this
schedule, your mileage would have peaked at 70 per week (113 km), and
you would have run your last 20-miler (32 km) on Sunday before the start
of the taper. Let’s go through this schedule day by day to see the rationale
for each day’s training.
