The first week of the taper starts with two days to recover from the long
run on Sunday. The second day includes six hill sprints and twelve 100-
meter strides to improve your leg turnover. Wednesday’s workout is a 9- to
10-miler (14 to 16 km) to provide a mild endurance stimulus.
Thursday and Friday are recovery days, leaving you well-rested for
Saturday when you run your last tune-up race before the marathon. The 8K
to 10K tune-up race provides a boost to your racing fitness that will make
marathon pace feel relatively easy, yet it is short enough that recovery will
occur quickly. Sunday is a 16- to 17-miler (26 to 27 km) to again provide a
moderate endurance stimulus to help maintain the adaptations, such as
increased glycogen storage and blood volume, from your previous long
runs. As discussed in chapter 1, having raced on Saturday, your long run on
Sunday should be at a more relaxed pace than your usual long runs.
The second week of the taper starts with three recovery days, with
Tuesday including 8 repetitions of 100-meter strides to enhance your leg
turnover. Thursday calls for your last workout at close to V O2max pace.
This is done 10 days before the marathon to allow time for recovery and
supercompensation. Friday and Saturday are recovery days, with Saturday
again including 100-meter strides to enhance your leg turnover. The week
ends with a 12- to 13-miler (19 to 21 km) on Sunday, once again providing
--- PAGE 206 ---
the body with a reminder to maintain the adaptations built up over many
weeks of long runs.
Marathon week is all easy recovery with the exception of Wednesday, a
dress rehearsal for the marathon. Wear the shoes, socks, shorts, and so on
that you’ll wear in the marathon. This is your last chance to check that
everything is right for the race. After an easy 3 miles (5 km), run 2 miles (3
km) at marathon race pace, then 1 to 2 more miles easy. You should feel
reasonably fresh but will probably not yet feel fully rested. If you have any
tight muscles, there’s still time to get a massage, stretch, use a foam roller,
or see a physical therapist to get your legs ready for race day.
By tapering in this way, you’ll reach the marathon start line as prepared
as possible. All that remains is the little matter of covering 26.2 miles (42.2
km) as fast as you can. Pacing and nutrition strategies to help you run your
best during the marathon are the focus of the next chapter.
TAPERING YOUR OTHER TRAINING
When you start cutting back your mileage, it can be tempting to increase
whatever nonrunning training you do. For the most part, try to resist that
urge. The various forms of supplementary training we looked at in chapter 4
have the same general purpose as your running training—to get you to the
marathon start line in optimal fitness for racing 26.2 miles (42.2 km).
Approach your core, resistance, flexibility, and cross-training as you do
your running when the marathon nears. That is, realize that the main work
is done, and your goal now shifts to maintaining the gains you’ve made
while allowing your body to rest up for race day.
WHERE’S THE PASTA FEED?
In chapter 2, we discussed the importance of carbohydrate
loading for the marathon. It’s important that your muscles and
liver are optimally stocked with glycogen when you reach the
start of the race. Of similar importance is arriving at the starting
line well hydrated. See chapter 2 for in-depth information on
these topics.
--- PAGE 207 ---
Keep your resistance workouts to a minimum in the last 10 days before
your marathon, and eliminate them in the last few days. It’s OK to continue
gentle versions of core training and cross-training until a few days before
the race. Flexibility training is fine to do right up to race day, but don’t go
overboard. If you’re used to a few 10-minute stretching sessions per week,
don’t suddenly devote an hour per day to it in the week before your
marathon.
The same goes for form drills. If you’re used to doing them, it’s fine to
continue with a short sequence in the week before the marathon. As with
flexibility training, the drills will help you feel looser during your taper,
which, if nothing else, can provide peace of mind while you’re reducing
your running. Again, don’t suddenly add new exercises or increase the
amount of drills you do during your taper.
SEEING SUCCESS
As race day approaches, it’s normal to feel anxious. The
marathon can seem more like a concept than a reality when it’s
10 weeks away, but when it’s just a matter of days, it can
become an all-too-real presence in your thoughts. That’s
especially true when you’re tapering your training and might be a
little more on edge as a result. And if you’re like many
marathoners, you’re worried that all the improvements made
during training will quickly evaporate after just a few rest days.
To keep your mind at ease, practice visualization during your
taper. Many of the world’s top runners consider race-week
visualization a key part of their final preparation. Use some of the
time you would usually allot to running—10 to 20 minutes per
day is a good amount—for this practice.
Start by sitting or lying in a quiet, comfortable spot, then
mentally run through your race in two ways. First, anticipate
potential problems. The goal isn’t to get more worked up about
your race. Rather, it’s to lessen the chance that you’re derailed
by an unfortunate development during the marathon. Visualize
things like getting boxed in during the early miles, missing an aid
--- PAGE 208 ---
station, inexplicably falling off pace, or challenging weather, then
picture yourself dealing with the problem and continuing with
your race plan. Visualize each potential problem in terms of, “If X
happens, then I’ll respond by doing Y.”
Second, visualize yourself running well at various parts of the
course. (This is easier to do if you’re familiar with the course or
have watched a video of it.) See yourself running relaxed in the
early stages of the marathon and then running strong over the
final 10K. Think about where in previous marathons the desire to
slow or stop might have cropped up. Acknowledge that
possibility, and then visualize yourself committing to bearing
down at that point. Finally, if you have a time goal for your
marathon, picture yourself hitting key splits en route and then
crossing the finish with the clock showing your goal time.
PRESERVING ENERGY (AND SANITY)
BEFORE THE MARATHON
If at all possible during the last week before the marathon, reduce not just
your training but also the amount of stress in your life. Assuming the
cooperation of your family, friends, boss, and coworkers, try to do the
following:
Avoid having major deadlines at work or other energy-draining
undertakings.
Wash your hands frequently to lower your risk of catching a cold or
other virus.
Get plenty of sleep early in the week, especially if you usually have
difficulty sleeping well in the few days before a big race.
Let others do the driving.
Minimize the amount of time you spend at the prerace expo.
Save sightseeing for after the marathon.
Spend a few minutes each day in a quiet spot visualizing a successful
race.
--- PAGE 209 ---
OceanofPDF.com
--- PAGE 210 ---
Y
7
RACE-DAY STRATEGY
our overall preparation for the marathon occurs over several months.
During that time, you meticulously plan and diligently train so
you’re in peak condition for the race. To do your best, however, you
also need to have a plan for the marathon itself. That plan is the focus of
this chapter. How much should you warm up for the marathon, and what
should that warm-up consist of? How should you handle the first few miles,
--- PAGE 211 ---
the first half of the race, the long stretch up to 20 miles (32 km), and the
final 6 miles and 385 yards (10 km)? Let’s take a look at race-day strategies
that help you get everything out of your months of preparation so you cross
the finish line exhausted but satisfied.
WARMING UP
Warming up for any race is important. The purpose of a warm-up is to
prepare your body to run at race pace. This involves increasing your
metabolic rate, your body temperature, and the circulation of blood (and
thus oxygen) to your muscles. The warm-up activates your aerobic system
to work optimally from the start of the race.
There’s a downside, however, to warming up for the marathon. One of
the challenges in the marathon is to reach the finish line before becoming
glycogen depleted. In chapter 2, we emphasized the importance of
carbohydrate loading in the several days before the marathon, having a
carbohydrate-rich prerace meal the morning of, and taking in carbohydrate
during the marathon to ensure that you don’t run out of carbohydrate before
the finish. But during a warm-up, you burn a mixture of carbohydrate and
fat, thereby slightly reducing your glycogen stores. The key, then, is to find
the minimum amount of warm-up necessary to prepare your body to handle
race pace as soon as the starter’s gun is fired so you save as much of your
carbohydrate reserves as possible for the 26.2 miles (42.2 km) ahead.
The optimal warm-up for the marathon depends on the level of the
marathoner. If your main goal is to finish, no warm-up is necessary. You can
warm up during the first couple miles of the race. If you will attempt to run
the distance significantly faster than normal training pace, you should do a
warm-up.
A typical marathon warm-up consists of the same dynamic flexibility
exercises you do before normal training runs, followed by about a mile (1.6
km) or 10 minutes of jogging and some light static stretching soon before
the start. That sequence accomplishes the basic goal of a warm-up, but for
the marathon we suggest a variation: 2 runs of about 4 to 5 minutes each
with some gentle stretching in between. This warm-up works well for the
marathon because you get a sense of how your body is feeling during the
--- PAGE 212 ---
first short run. You can then focus the rest of your warm-up on any minor
adjustments so you feel as good as possible when you line up for the start.
You should start warming up about 30 to 40 minutes before the start of
the race. After some dynamic flexibility exercises, start your first warm-up
run slowly, and gradually increase your pace so you finish at about 1 minute
per mile (37 seconds per km) slower than marathon race pace. Next, do
some stretching for 5 to 10 minutes, including loosening up your shoulders
and neck. Follow that with another 4 to 5 minutes of running, this time
gradually picking up the pace until you reach marathon pace for the final 30
seconds or so. On a warm day, you may want to drink a few ounces before
heading to the starting area.
That’s it. Try to time your warm-up so you finish no more than 10
minutes before the race starts. If you warm up too long before the race,
you’ll lose some of the benefits of the warm-up yet will have still used up
some of your carbohydrate stores. The ability to time your warm-up like
this is an advantage of running a smaller marathon, as compared with a
megarace where you’re more likely to be herded to your starting position
long before the race begins.
Before the start of the Olympic marathon, the athletes do a bit of nervous
jogging around, but almost no one does more than 10 minutes of easy
running plus one or two accelerations up to race pace. This is enough of a
warm-up for these runners to handle 5-minutes-per-mile pace for the first
mile. A similar routine will get you to the starting line prepared to handle
your goal marathon race pace.
YOUR PACING STRATEGY
Assuming that you have a time goal for the marathon, how should you go
about trying to achieve that time? Some marathoners go out hard and then
try to hang on as well as possible in the second half. Others try to run an
even pace throughout. A few take it easy early on and then run the second
half faster. Let’s consider the physiology of the marathon and the
implications for your optimal pacing strategy.
In chapter 1, we saw that your marathon pace is very close to your
lactate-threshold (LT) pace, which is determined by your oxygen
--- PAGE 213 ---
consumption at your LT and your running economy. If you run faster than
your LT pace, lactate accumulates in your muscles and blood; do this for
more than a few minutes, and the hydrogen ions associated with the lactate
begin to deactivate the enzymes for energy production and make you slow
down. When you exceed your LT pace, you also use more glycogen, so your
limited glycogen stores are depleted more quickly than necessary.
These basics of marathon physiology indicate that the best strategy for
the marathon is relatively even pacing. If you run much faster than your
overall race pace for part of the race, you’ll use more glycogen than
necessary and will likely start to accumulate lactate. If you run much slower
than your overall race pace for part of the race, you’ll need to make up for
this lapse by running faster than the most efficient pace for another portion
of the race. The optimal pacing strategy, then, is to run nearly even splits,
taking into account the idiosyncrasies of the course you’ll be running.
Most runners shouldn’t try to run dead-even splits, however, because
during the marathon you’ll gradually fatigue your slow-twitch muscle fibers
and will start to recruit more of your fast-twitch A fibers to maintain your
pace. Unfortunately, these fast-twitch fibers tend to be less economical than
your slow-twitch fibers in their use of oxygen. Therefore, your running
economy will tend to decrease slightly during the race, meaning that your
LT pace will decrease slightly as well. The result is that your optimal pace
will be slightly reduced during the later stages of the marathon.
For example, if your goal is to run 2:39 for the marathon, even splits
would require you to run 1:19:30 for each half of the race. To run even
splits, you would have to increase your oxygen consumption and lactate
level as your fatigue level increases during the second half of the race. A
more efficient pacing strategy would be to go through halfway in 1:18 to
1:19 because doing so would allow you to slow by 2 to 3 percent during the
second half and still achieve your goal. If you ran negative splits for the
marathon (i.e., the second half faster than the first half), chances are that
you ran more slowly than optimally during the first half of the race and
could have had a faster finishing time.
Some runners have reported that they’re better able to hold even pace
throughout the marathon when racing in super shoes. (See the “What Shoes
Should You Wear?” sidebar.) It’s impossible to know for how many runners
in super shoes that’s true and why exactly it might happen. One obvious
--- PAGE 214 ---
explanation is that running in super shoes might lead to less of the muscle
damage that can contribute to late-race slowing. This idea is supported by
the common perception of not feeling as beat up during and after a long run
in super shoes compared to conventional trainers. It’s also possible that,
because of enabling quicker recovery, super shoes can lead to better
preparation and, therefore, less of a tendency to fade over the final miles of
a marathon. If you’ve run marathons in super shoes and found yourself
finishing faster than you’ve run marathons in conventional shoes, this might
be a sign that you could adopt a more aggressive even-splits strategy.
For world-class marathoners, whose genetics and training put them on a
higher plane, the optimal pacing strategy is likely a bit different. These
select few are so highly trained that they have a lower tendency to recruit
less economical muscle fibers as the race progresses. In addition, they can
pick up the pace over the last several miles and gradually accumulate
lactate to the finish. For the best marathoners in the world, therefore, the
most effective pacing strategy is to run the second half of the marathon at
the same pace as, or even slightly faster than, the first half.
Most of the recent world records have followed this model of faster
second halves. In setting the world best of 2:00:35 at the 2023 Chicago
Marathon, Kelvin Kiptum ran 60:48 for the first half and 59:47 for the
second half. Similarly, in her world record at the 2023 Berlin Marathon,
Tigst Assefa ran 2:11:53 by running the first half in 66:20 and the second
half in 65:33.
Perhaps you’ll find it reassuring that even the best runners in the world
pay for it when they start a marathon too quickly. At the 2024 Tokyo
Marathon, Eliud Kipchoge attempted to reclaim the title of world-record
holder from Kiptum. Following overeager pacers, Kipchoge ran 28:30 for
the first 10K and passed 15K in 42:52—fast enough to win most open 10K
and 15K road races. Kipchoge slowed slightly but reached halfway in
60:34, faster than Kiptum’s first half in his record run. Still, Kipchoge had
already lost contact with the trio who would eventually place first, second,
and third, and he faded significantly after that. His second half took 66:16,
resulting in a finishing time of 2:06:50. Runners who had started more
moderately overtook Kipchoge, and the two-time Olympic champion placed
tenth, his lowest placing in any of his marathons.
--- PAGE 215 ---
Less dramatically, when Ruth Chepngetich ran her barrier-breaking
2:09:56 at the 2024 Chicago Marathon, she covered the first half in 64:16
and the second half in 65:40. It might sound silly to say so, but it’s possible
she could have broken Assefa’s world record by even more with a slightly
more conservative first half.
Eliud Kipchoge
Fastest marathon: 2:01:09 (Berlin, 2022)
Marathon highlights: champion in the 2016 and 2021
Olympics; 5-time Berlin, 4-time London, 1-time Tokyo, and 1-
time Chicago winner; world record holder in 2018 and 2022;
fastest marathon runner under any condition (1:59:40 at the
2019 INEOS Challenge)
We’re among those who consider Eliud Kipchoge to be the
greatest marathoner in history. That’s a pretty easy call
concerning someone who has won two Olympic titles, twice set
the world record, went undefeated for seven years, set course
records at three World Marathon Majors and, for good
measure, became the first person to run under two hours for
26.2 miles (albeit in an exhibition race not eligible for record
purposes).
--- PAGE 216 ---
Description
So, what can those of us who aren’t considered the greatest
marathoner in history learn from Kipchoge?
Kipchoge obviously has supreme physical gifts—you don’t
dominate as a marathoner and win Olympic and world
championship medals on the track otherwise. One of his key
talents is the ability to hold up to world-class training. Kipchoge
hasn’t suffered an injury significant enough to keep him from a
marathon start line. Remaining healthy has allowed him to bring
fitness from previous training cycles to underlie his current
work.
Still, we suspect some of his competitors would match or
exceed some of Kipchoge’s physiological measurements in lab
tests. What has really separated Kipchoge from the pack is his
mindset. That’s an area all marathoners can look to for time
gains.
--- PAGE 217 ---
“If you don’t rule your mind, it can rule you,” Kipchoge has
said. “I tell my mind that I’m really able to do this thing. And I
will do it.”
Kipchoge isn’t delusional—he hasn’t targeted a 1:30
marathon. What he’s talking about is being open to the
possibility that, with the right physical preparation, great things
can happen on race day if his mind is in the right place. Such a
mindset is more sophisticated than thrashing yourself and
otherwise ignoring your body’s signals. It’s about learning how
to work through the inevitable tough patches—in training and
racing—so that you stay positive, relaxed, and efficient. There’s
a growing body of research showing that concentrating on
staying relaxed not only diverts your mind from unpleasant
topics but also slightly boosts your running economy at a given
pace.
Kipchoge practices prerace visualization; he forms concrete
images of winning and running great times as well as scenarios
in which he overcomes setbacks. He has an arsenal of
personally meaningful mantras he repeats to himself in racing
and training that motivate him to stay focused on his goals.
Another key to Kipchoge’s psychological approach is that he
loves the process of preparing for a marathon. This isn’t to say
that every run is an endorphin rush for him or that you should
be giddy every time you wake early to run 12 miles before
work. Kipchoge’s take is akin to that of Olympic medalist Deena
Kastor, who talks about making choices, not sacrifices. That is,
view what you do to further your running as contributions to
your big-picture goal of running the best marathon you’re
capable of. For Kipchoge, that includes spending time in
training camp away from his family. For you, that could be
putting a positive spin on matters such as what you eat and
how often you do strength training.
As he entered his late 30s, Kipchoge’s air of invincibility
started to ebb. He finished eighth at London in 2020, the lowest
placing of his career at the time. He rebounded—and then
some!—by successfully defending his Olympic title in 2021, and
--- PAGE 218 ---
in 2022, he set a course record at Tokyo and a world record at
Berlin. But then he finished sixth at Boston in 2023. He won
Berlin that fall then had a disastrous (for him) 2024, placing
tenth at Tokyo and dropping out of the Olympic marathon, the
only DNF of his career.
There are lessons we can learn from the later part of
Kipchoge’s career. Before Boston, Kipchoge said that he didn’t
prepare specifically for the famously undulating course. He
started at course-record pace but then lost contact with the lead
pack in the Newton hills when the real racing began.
Apparently, even the greatest marathoner in history can’t ignore
the principle of training and racing appropriately for the course.
Kipchoge’s overall struggles as he neared age 40 highlight
the need for long-time marathoners to be open to new
approaches. Throughout his marathon career, Kipchoge has
immediately put himself near the front of the lead pack and
dared others to try to keep up. Some days, that still works for
him. But it’s not uncommon for runners to experience more day-
to-day variability in how they feel as they age. Continuing to
use the same tactics (and, for that matter, training) that worked
a decade earlier can backfire. For someone like Kipchoge, that
might mean that trying to win the 2024 Tokyo race caused him
to overextend himself and place tenth, when on that day in that
field he might have been able to place, say, third with a more
measured strategy. Being more flexible in this regard should be
easier for those of us not trying to add to an already historic
marathoning CV.
Sifan Hassan
Fastest marathon: 2:13:24 (Chicago, 2023)
Marathon highlights: first in the 2024 Olympics, the 2023
Chicago Marathon, and the 2023 London Marathon
--- PAGE 219 ---
Olympic champions are, by definition, outliers. But Sifan
Hassan is an outlier even among those outliers.
Description
Hassan first became widely known beyond hardcore running
fans at the 2021 Olympics, where she won the 5,000- and
10,000-meter titles and took bronze at 1,500 meters. Even
more improbable, at the 2024 Olympics, she earned bronze at
5,000 and 10,000 meters, and then she outsprinted the world-
record holder to win the marathon—just 36 hours after racing
the 10,000 meters in spikes! We’re also unaware of any other
runner who, like Hassan at London in 2023, won their debut
marathon against a world-class field despite stopping twice to
stretch their quads and repeatedly losing contact with the lead
pack.
Nonetheless, some key lessons emerge from studying
Hassan’s career.
First, Hassan hasn’t yet fallen into a trap that has snared
other track racers who are immediate successes in the
marathon. Some such runners arguably succeed in their first
one or two marathons because of, not despite, their recent
history of lots of V O2max and basic speed workouts. Marathon
race pace feels quite comfortable in comparison. But some
--- PAGE 220 ---
then tell themselves, “I’m a marathoner now,” and pile on more
volume and long tempo runs at the expense of shorter, faster
sessions. There are many examples of elites making this shift
and never improving on their initial marathons.
Hassan, in contrast, trains and races at a wide variety of
paces and distances, even when preparing for a marathon.
Doing so keeps her fresh physically and mentally. While you
won’t find “run a track 10K in spikes 36 hours before your goal
marathon” in any of our training schedules, we do encourage
you to keep in touch with a wide range of intensities and to not
avoid shorter races. Hassan’s race goals are dictated in part by
the international competition schedule. Yours can be a little
more flexible, such as targeting a fall marathon after a spring
season devoted to racing no farther than 5K or 10K.
Second, Hassan sets seemingly audacious goals as
motivators. (If nothing else, her goals require that she do the
truly varied training discussed above.) “It was really amazing,”
Hassan said after winning London in her debut. “I never thought
I would finish a marathon.” This is an overstatement—Hassan
could have stepped out her door any day and run 26.2 miles.
She meant racing a marathon against the best of the world
after spending the bulk of her career racing 1,500 meters on
the track. Goals that are a little scary are good because they
motivate you to do the work to meet them. She expressed
similar concerns before her 2024 Olympic triple. “It’s my crazy
thing,” she said. “I just want to complete it. I’m very curious.
Could I podium? Could I even complete? Am I strong enough?”
Finally, Hassan acknowledges that she’s aiming high but
doesn’t let her goals incapacitate her. “I always think, ‘Why the
hell do I do this to myself?’” she said between the 2024
Olympic Opening Ceremony and her first race, the qualifying
round of the 5,000 meters. On the start line, she often appears
absolutely miserable, her face frozen in a look of, “How did I get
myself into this mess?” She seems to calm herself by
momentarily lowering her expectations. In her track races, she
almost always goes immediately to the back of the pack, as if to
--- PAGE 221 ---
convince herself she’s not really racing yet, that she can just
hang out for however many laps and then get down to
business. Before the marathon, it’s via the dubious claim that
she just wants to finish.
Most marathoners feel similarly overwhelmed by the enormity
of the task ahead if they dwell on it. If that’s you, find ways to
channel your thoughts elsewhere. Then, once your race starts,
do like Hassan, and let your instincts, experience, and
preparation take over.
ALTERING YOUR STRATEGY
MIDRACE
Although in most cases you should stay with your pacing plan, occasionally
the weather or the tactics of other runners merit slightly altering your
strategy. If you’re running into a headwind, there’s a substantial advantage
to running in a group and letting others block the wind. Though you may
need to do your share in leading the group, you’ll still save considerable
energy compared with running by yourself into the wind. On a windy day,
therefore, you may need to run moderately faster or slower than planned to
stay with a group.
Even on a calm day, the best strategy may be to deviate slightly from
your goal pace rather than running most of the way by yourself. In a big-
city marathon such as Boston, New York, or Chicago, being stuck by
yourself isn’t a problem. At almost any pace, you’ll be among a number of
runners, and you can work with them to reach your goal time. In a smaller
marathon, however, you have a reasonably high chance of running by
yourself for many miles. In that situation, you must make a judgment call as
to whether to go a few seconds per mile faster or slower than planned to
stay with a group. Although drafting behind other runners will give you a
small energy advantage, most of the benefit of staying with a group is
psychological. You don’t have to set the pace, and you can relax and go
along with the group.
--- PAGE 222 ---
Most runners find it quite difficult mentally to run by themselves for
long stretches of the marathon. So what’s the trade-off between having
company and having to compromise your strategy? A rule of thumb is to
deviate from your goal pace by no more than 8 to 10 seconds per mile (1.6
km) if you would otherwise be running by yourself during the first 20 miles
(32 km) of the race. (Of course, you won’t know until afterward whether
you would have had to run by yourself.) Running 8 to 10 seconds per mile
faster than planned may not sound like much, but this difference in effort
can put you over the edge after a couple of miles. The best way to judge
whether to pick it up to latch onto a group is by how you feel at the time. If
you feel as though you can handle it, go for it. If your breathing is
uncomfortable and you can sense that you’re working at a higher intensity
than you can maintain until the finish, relax and let the others go. You may
find that the group soon breaks up and that you’ll once again have others
with whom to run.
ADAPTING TO HEAT AND HUMIDITY
If you’ve been training in cool conditions and it will be warm or
hot for your marathon, it pays to prepare in advance. If you’ll be
running a marathon in high humidity when you live in a low-
humidity environment, it’s even more important to prepare
because the body adapts differently to hot and humid conditions
than to hot and dry conditions.
As discussed in chapter 2, the evaporation of sweat is a key
cooling mechanism for the body. With high humidity, the air
contains more moisture and therefore less sweat evaporates
from your skin. You sweat more (and lose more fluid), but it’s less
effective in maintaining your body temperature. The result is that
your core temperature rises more quickly and you eventually
need to slow.
Even if race day starts at a mild 55 degrees Fahrenheit (13
degrees Celsius) and only increases to 65 degrees Fahrenheit
(18 degrees Celsius), a change from low humidity in training to a
--- PAGE 223 ---
high humidity marathon often catches runners out and leads to a
disappointing performance.
You can get used to warm conditions or warm and humid
conditions by doing some of your training on a treadmill (without
a fan to reduce evaporative cooling) or wearing more clothes
than you normally would during training runs. Allowing yourself to
be warmer than usual (uncomfortably warm but not drained)
during training will provide a stimulus for your body to adapt to
warmer conditions. Wearing more clothes during running
simulates high humidity by trapping heat next to your skin and
increasing your sweat rate and your core temperature. For
example, you could wear a long sleeve top, tights, and a cap
when you would ordinarily run in a t-shirt and shorts. To get the
most adaptation, use these two strategies as consistently as
possible over the final two to three weeks before your marathon.
If you have to do your final long run or tempo run somewhat
slower than planned, that’s okay because you’ll be simulating,
and therefore adapting to, race-day conditions.
On a warm and humid day or a day that’s just plain hot for your
marathon, stopping at aid stations rather than running through
them and drinking more fluid than you would in cooler conditions
will help you get through the race, but you’ll likely also need to
adjust your time goal. How much to adjust your time goal
depends on how hot and humid the day will be and how
challenging your target time is. Also, some runners are simply
better heat runners than others, regardless of their acclimation.
In general, err on the side of what might feel overly conservative
for at least the first half. Rather than starting with a wait-and-see
attitude or assuming that you’ll be able to gut it out, it pays to
adjust your marathon goal in advance and start out at a realistic
pace for the conditions.
During the final 6 miles and 385 yards (10 km), you can afford to be
more independent. If no one else is running at the correct pace for you after
you’ve passed the 20-mile (32 km) mark, you need to muster the courage to
--- PAGE 224 ---
go it alone. Chances are that forging out will work well psychologically
because if you have prepared well and run a fairly even pace, you’ll be
passing other runners throughout the final miles. Nothing lifts the spirits
quite like passing another runner late in the marathon.
WHAT ABOUT ORGANIZED PACE
GROUPS?
Many big marathons offer pacers to help guide runners toward
specific times. Unlike the pacers elites use, these pacers go the
whole marathon distance. Should you use this service?
It depends.
First, of course, is the matter of whether your marathon
provides a pacer at or near your goal time. Pacers for sub-3:00
times are rare. Bigger races often provide pacers in 5-minute
increments (e.g., 3:10, 3:15, 3:20, etc.), while pacers at smaller
marathons might be available in 10- or 15-minute increments.
The more pace-group options there are, the more it’s worth
considering joining one.
It’s rare for pacers to not finish near their target times. Most
are capable of running a marathon significantly faster; their
pacing assignment is usually at an easy to moderate long-run
effort for them. It can be comforting to tell yourself that if you
stick with this person, you’re almost guaranteed to finish in the
targeted time.
One big consideration is the topography of your marathon
course. Pacers tend to aim for an even pace, regardless of a
course’s hills, turns, and weather. Sometimes those factors
warrant even effort rather than even pace in the first two-thirds of
a marathon. Following an even pace in these situations might not
result in your best possible time that day.
Another item to consider is how comfortable you are running in
a potentially large group. If you’ve done most of your marathon
training solo or with one or two others, it can be jarring to
--- PAGE 225 ---
suddenly be part of a tight pack of 30 people running the same
pace. There might be a lot of talking, especially in the early
miles. You might have a harder time getting a clear shot at fluids
at aid stations. Whether being part of a big group is appealing or
a turnoff is an individual matter.
Our general recommendation: If there’s a pace group available
near your goal time, consider starting with it. Doing so is a good
way to ensure you don’t run the early miles too quickly. If you find
the group distracting, drift away from it. And if you’re having a
great day, don’t be afraid to strike out ahead over the last 10K.
THE FIRST HALF
You’re finally at the starting line, warmed up, and ready for the task ahead.
It’s all too easy to get carried away and run the first mile (1.6 km) too fast.
A better approach is to run the first mile at or a bit slower than your goal
pace. Because you won’t have done much of a warm-up before the start,
your body won’t be prepared to go faster than race pace. Also, if you run
too fast at the beginning of the race, your body will burn off extra glycogen
and start to accumulate lactate that could negatively affect the rest of your
race.
Once the first mile is out of the way, the best strategy during the next
few miles is to settle into a good rhythm. Try to run fast but relaxed.
Establishing a relaxed running style early in the race will go a long way
toward helping you avoid tightening up so you can maintain your goal pace
to the finish. Go through a mental checklist periodically to make sure your
shoulders are relaxed, your body is upright, and so on to help you maintain
good running style throughout the race.
WHAT SHOES SHOULD YOU WEAR?
Marathoners used to have to choose between lightweight,
minimally cushioned racing flats and heavier, more cushioned
shoes on race day. The tradeoff was saving weight (racing flats)
versus having more cushioning and support over the final miles
--- PAGE 226 ---
as fatigue and muscular damage accumulated (trainers). Many
competitive marathoners opted for the middle ground of a
lightweight trainer. As with much else in running, super shoes
have upended this old quandary.
We’ll define super shoes as models made primarily for racing
half marathons and marathons with these features:
A large amount of next-generation, bouncy midsole foam
A carbon fiber plate or other stiffening agent in the midsole
to provide stability given the large amount of foam
underfoot
A rockered geometry that helps to move the foot toward
toe-off
A relatively light weight—usually around 8 ounces (227 g)
or less for men and 7 ounces (198 g) or less for women—to
improve running economy
Research has found a wide range of improvements in running
economy among runners in super shoes versus conventional
shoes at the same pace. Faster runners tend to see a bit more
improvement, presumably because they’re putting more force
into the ground and therefore get more of a response from super
shoes’ design features. But slower runners still tend to get a 1 or
2 percent boost (Joubert, Dominy, and Burns 2023).
It can be difficult to find the right super shoe for you. You can’t
know which combination of midsole softness, plate positioning,
and shoe geometry works best for your running mechanics
without running at race pace. (Super shoes are awkward to walk
and even jog in, so there’s little to learn from a brief try-on.) Also,
super shoes are the most expensive shoes on the market; most
runners can’t afford to truly test several models.
Some runners, including a few sponsored elites, simply don’t
like the feeling of running in super shoes. The large amount of
foam underfoot can be challenging for runners with poor ankle
mobility. Most shoe brands offer what they call a “training
companion” to their premier racing shoes. These shoes
--- PAGE 227 ---
incorporate super shoe elements, such as modern midsole foam
and rockered geometry, in a less aggressive, slightly heavier
shoe. And if you’ve been able to meet your marathoning goals in
conventional trainers and have tried but didn’t like super shoes,
or if you simply can’t justify spending hundreds of dollars on
shoes that are good for only 150-200 miles, there’s no shame in
continuing to wear what works for you.
Regardless of which shoes you choose for race day, be sure to
try them out on tempo runs, at least one of your longer
marathon-pace training runs, and the short dress-rehearsal run
during taper week.
In chapter 2 we discussed having a plan for your fluid and carbohydrate
intake during the marathon. Glitches will arise, but do your best to adhere to
your plan for taking gels and drinks. Take a carbohydrate drink at the initial
aid station. It’s important to drink right from the start rather than wait until
you think you need carbohydrate or fluid. If you wait until you feel tired
and lightheaded to take in carbohydrate, it will be too late. Taking in
carbohydrate and fluid early will help reduce dehydration and postpone or
prevent carbohydrate depletion, which means you will be able to maintain
your goal pace longer. As we discussed in chapter 2, a few seconds lost at
each aid station can translate into several minutes gained toward the end of
the marathon.
Mentally, the first half of the marathon is the time to cruise. Try to save
your mental and emotional energy for the second half of the race. All other
factors being equal, if there is a group of runners in the lead pack at
halfway, the winner will be the one who has cruised along at the back of the
pack saving energy for the demands of the second half of the race. The
same principle holds true for slower packs as well. Regardless of your
ultimate finishing place in the marathon, you should realize that the second
half is going to be harder than the first half; just try to get the first half out
of the way at the correct pace without using any more mental energy than
necessary.
--- PAGE 228 ---
CHANGING TIME ZONES
If you’re traveling across the country or internationally for your
marathon, you need to consider the change in time zones. You
can prepare your body for that change by adjusting your sleep
and eating patterns to the new time zone for a few days before
you travel.
It’s easier for your brain to adjust to traveling west and
extending your day than it is to travel east and shorten your day.
Prior to westward travel, get used to getting up and going to bed
somewhat later than usual. This can be difficult with a job and
other commitments, but you should be able to adjust by an hour
or two in this way. Upon arrival, if you’ve traveled west for up to
four time zones, you can simply have a nap early in the day and
go to sleep at a normal time in the new time zone.
If you’re traveling east for your marathon, such as for New
York City or Boston, you should allow a day to adjust for each
hour change in time zone. For example, when traveling from the
West Coast to the East Coast for a marathon on Sunday, plan to
arrive no later than Thursday. For a few days prior to eastward
travel, try getting up earlier and going to bed earlier than usual
and adjusting your meals accordingly. This can be difficult in
practice, but you should be able to adjust by an hour or so. Upon
arrival, you may struggle going to bed on the earlier East Coast
time, but after three nights you should have adapted to the new
time reasonably well. This is particularly important if your
marathon has an early morning start.
A few other tips:
Set your watch and phone to the new time zone while on
the plane to mentally adjust
Avoid caffeine late in the day
Limit alcohol consumption, which interferes with sleep,
when traveling and upon arrival
--- PAGE 229 ---
When you arrive, go outside in the daylight to help your
brain adjust to the new time zone
If you have a nap, keep it under 45 minutes and wake up by
early afternoon
ON TO 20 MILES (32 KM)
From the halfway mark to 20 miles (32 km) is the no man’s land of the
marathon. You’re already fairly tired and still have a long way to go. This is
where the mental discipline of training will help you maintain a strong
effort and a positive attitude. It’s easy to let your pace slip 5 seconds per
mile (1.6 km), and then 10 seconds per mile or more, during this stretch. By
using all the available feedback on your pace—whether in the form of mile
or kilometer splits—you’ll know exactly how you’re progressing, and you
should be able to concentrate and maintain your goal pace during these
miles.
Slowing during this portion of the marathon is often more a matter of not
concentrating than of not being able to physically maintain the pace.
Focusing on your splits gives you an immediate goal to concentrate on. If
you’re consulting your GPS watch, bear in mind that its mile or kilometer
marks might vary slightly from the race’s markers, owing to how road race
courses are measured and the usual variances in GPS accuracy. Use the
race’s markers as your main pace barometers because your official final
time will reflect the course measurement, not what your watch says.
If you do slow during one of these miles, don’t suddenly increase your
pace to get back on track. If you’re using your GPS’s current-pace data, aim
to get back to your goal pace. When the next mile split confirms you’re
back on pace, chip away at the few lost seconds over the next few miles. By
focusing on these incremental goals along the way, you’ll prevent a large
drift in your pace and should be able to stay very close to your goal.
It’s not unusual to have a few miles when you just don’t feel good. These
bad patches are a test of mental resolve. Often these stretches will last a
while and then mysteriously go away. For example, you might feel tight and
uncomfortable from miles 15 to 17 but then get back in the groove again
--- PAGE 230 ---
and feel good to the finish. The key is to have the confidence that you’ll
eventually overcome this bad patch.
WHEN NOT TO FINISH
Most of the time, you should finish the marathon even if you’re
not running as well as you had hoped. The marathon is a test of
endurance. If you casually drop out of a marathon once, it will be
all too easy to drop out again. Of course, in certain situations,
struggling to finish a marathon may compromise your health or
your future marathon success. Here are some scenarios where
dropping out is your best choice:
If you’re limping, your running mechanics are thrown off.
You’ll merely aggravate your injury by continuing.
If you have a specific pain, and that pain is increasing
progressively during the race, you’re doing yourself harm
and should stop.
If you’re lightheaded and unable to concentrate, you should
stop.
If you’re overcome by muscle cramps, a torn muscle, heat
exhaustion, or the like, stop.
Pete learned this lesson years ago during the San Francisco Marathon.
After working quite hard from miles 13 to 16, he had a stretch of about 3
miles when his breathing felt out of sync on a particularly windy stretch of
road, and he struggled to stay with the other two leaders. Pete kept telling
himself to relax and that the other guys might be hurting too. Fortunately,
he settled back into a comfortable rhythm by 19 miles, felt strong enough to
drop the other two runners by the 20-mile mark, and cruised home to
victory. If Pete had let himself think negatively during the bad patch and let
the other two runners get away, he wouldn’t have won that race.
--- PAGE 231 ---
DROPPING OUT
In my marathon career, I started 18 marathons and finished 16,
including 8 victories. Of the two dropouts, one was because of
injury and the other was because of stupidity. Both occurred in
1986.
The dropout because of stupidity was the Boston Marathon.
This was the first professional Boston Marathon, with lots of
media excitement and financial incentives. I ignored my usual
race plan of running even splits and got carried away early.
During the first 10 miles, 1983 Boston Marathon champion Greg
Meyer and I exchanged the lead several times. When Greg
would take the lead, I would try to take it back.
This was overly aggressive racing on my part for so early in
the marathon. Meanwhile, Rob de Castella, who won that day,
sat in behind us, probably smiling to himself at the lack of
patience of the two Americans. By 12 miles, my breathing wasn’t
in its usual rhythm, my legs were already pretty beat up, and my
intestines were becoming increasingly uncomfortable. During the
next mile, I fell off the lead bunch and started to tighten up.
Knowing that I had gone too hard too early, I stopped just past
the halfway mark and quietly cursed myself. It was a lesson well
learned and a mistake I never made again.
The only positive aspect of dropping out so early was that I
recovered fully in about a week. A few weeks later, I used my
fitness and frustration to set a personal best of 28:41 for 10,000
meters on the track, and in July I redeemed myself over 26.2
miles by winning the San Francisco Marathon in 2:13:29.
My other dropout was in the 1986 Twin Cities Marathon, which
was also the trial race for the next year’s world championships. I
went into the race with a tight hamstring from stretching too hard
after a track workout several weeks earlier. It felt OK early in the
race but gradually tightened up in the drizzly 40-degree
Fahrenheit (4 degrees Celsius) weather. At 20 miles, I was at the
back of the lead pack when my hamstring tightened completely. I
--- PAGE 232 ---
couldn’t run another step. After walking for about a mile, I
gratefully accepted a ride to the finish. That dropout was easier
mentally because the decision was out of my hands.
Having had a frustrating year in the marathon, I was
determined to get a decent race under my belt as quickly as
possible. My physical therapist said that the hamstring wasn’t too
badly damaged and that I should run easily for about 10 days.
With regular massage, it loosened up, and I decided to run the
New York City Marathon, which was 3 weeks after Twin Cities. I
ran conservatively and gradually moved from 30th place at
halfway to finish ninth in 2:14:09.
I used those lessons to finish third running even splits in the
1987 New York City Marathon in 2:11:54 and five months later
running a strong last 10 miles to move up from seventh to third in
the 1988 U.S. Olympic Trials Marathon.
—Pete Pfitzinger
Bad patches between halfway and 20 miles can also stem from
encroaching muscular fatigue. Do periodic body scans to see if you’re
starting to run less efficiently. Go from top to bottom, focusing on one
element of good form at a time. Is your head lined up over your shoulders?
Are you relaxed in the face and neck? Are your shoulders low and even?
Are your hands loosely cupped? Is your trunk tall and not bent over at the
waist? Are your hips relaxed? Are your feet landing under rather than ahead
of your center of mass?
You can do these scans in just a few seconds. If an area feels tight or out
of alignment, envision how it felt in the early, relatively easy miles, and try
to regain that form. Fixing these small form deviations will make the
current stretch feel a little easier and will lessen the chance of an even
bigger form breakdown late in the race.
You don’t need to constantly monitor your form. A quick check every
mile or so is plenty. Keep doing these body scans the rest of the race, both
to maintain your technique and to provide a different psychological focus.
Taking in carbohydrate as often as possible during the second half of the
race can help you maintain your mental focus. The only fuel for your brain
--- PAGE 233 ---
is glucose (carbohydrate), and when you become carbohydrate depleted, the
amount of glucose reaching the brain starts to decrease. If you’ve
carbohydrate-loaded, this shouldn’t start to affect you until well past the 20-
mile (32 km) mark. As discussed in chapter 2, taking in carbohydrate (in
fluids and gels) during the race and particularly during the second half will
help ensure that you stay alert and think clearly throughout the race.
THE FINAL 6 MILES AND 385 YARDS
(10 KM)
Having made it to 20 miles (32 km), you’re at the most rewarding stage of
the marathon. This is the part that you have prepared for during your long
months of training. This is when your long runs, during which you worked
hard over the last stages, will really pay off. Until now, everything required
the patience to hold back. Now, you’re free to see what you’ve got. During
these final 6 miles and 385 yards (10 km), you get to dig deep and use up
any energy that you have left. This is what the marathon is all about. This is
the stretch that poorly prepared marathoners fear and well-prepared
marathoners relish.
The key from 20 miles (32 km) to the finish is to push as hard as you can
without having disaster strike in the form of a cramp or very tight muscles.
You will have prepared yourself for this during your long runs, your
marathon-pace runs, and, to a lesser extent, your tempo runs. You need to
use your body’s feedback to determine just how hard you can push.
Chances are that by now, your calf muscles, hamstrings, quads, or some
combination of these are on edge and will limit how fast you can go. You
need to test the waters a bit and push to what you perceive to be the limit
that your muscles will tolerate. This is a progressive meting-out process in
which you can take progressively greater risks as the finish line nears.
Although doing the how-many-miles-to-go math can be daunting early
on in the marathon, in this final stage it can help keep you focused. As the
finish approaches, telling yourself, “Less than 3 miles to go,” or “Just 15
minutes more,” can be motivating. If you’re struggling a bit toward the end,
picture yourself finishing a run on your favorite training loop so the
remaining distance seems more manageable.
--- PAGE 234 ---
If you’ve been taking in fluid and carbohydrate throughout the race, your
muscles should be in pretty good condition. Keep drinking until 25 miles
(40 km). Keeping up your blood sugar level will keep you alert so you can
concentrate well to the end. If your stomach is bothering you in the final
miles, use the rinse-and-spit drink technique discussed in chapter 2.
When you see the finish line approaching, give a little more effort so you
run strongly over the line. Show yourself that you have mastered the
marathon and are able to kick it in a bit to the finish. Then enjoy the fruits
of your labor.
In these first several chapters, we’ve looked at preparing for and running
the marathon from all the necessary angles. Now it is time to put theory into
practice. The rest of this book contains training schedules that implement
the physiological principles of marathoning. The knowledge gained from
this first section of the book combined with the marathon-specific fitness
that following the schedules will bring you should leave you well prepared
for marathon success.
OceanofPDF.com
--- PAGE 235 ---
PART II
Training Programs
OceanofPDF.com
--- PAGE 236 ---
A
8
FOLLOWING THE SCHEDULES
s we noted in the preface, many readers want to get right to the
training schedule of their choice and start working. That’s fine, but
before getting too far into your training, you’ll want to read this
chapter. In it, you’ll learn the best way to do each of the run types called for
in the schedules. You’ll also see how to tailor the mileage in the schedules
--- PAGE 237 ---
to your capacity and what to do when the almost inevitable roadblocks pop
up during your training.
The training schedules in chapters 9 through 13 include a specific
workout for each day as well as a category of training for that day. Many of
the workouts provide a range of distance so that you can tailor the session
based on how you’re feeling that day. For example, a recovery run may
specify a range of 5 to 7 miles, a general aerobic run may call for 8 to 10
miles, and a medium-long run may be 12 to 14 miles. By selecting the
distance that feels right for your energy level, you fulfill the training
purpose for the day while listening to your body.
The workouts are divided into eight categories: long runs, medium-long
runs, marathon-pace runs, lactate-threshold (LT) runs, general aerobic runs,
recovery runs, V O2max intervals, and speed training. Each run is explained
here, including how to get the most benefit from a given workout. For an
in-depth explanation of the physiological benefit and role of each type of
training, see chapter 1.
LONG RUNS
In the training schedules, a long run is any run of 16 miles (26 km) or
longer. The intention of long runs is (obviously) to improve your endurance
in preparation for the marathon’s 26.2 miles (42.2 km).
To gain the most from your long runs, do them in the correct intensity
range. Long runs shouldn’t be slow jogs during which you just accumulate
time on your feet. As discussed in chapter 1, the appropriate pace for a
specific long run depends on the purpose of that run within your training
program. The most beneficial intensity range for most of your long runs is
10 to 20 percent slower than your goal marathon race pace. For most
marathoners, long-run pace coincides with about 75 to 83 percent of
maximal heart rate or 66 to 77 percent of heart rate reserve. In terms of your
breathing, this effort should feel about the same or slightly harder than a
general aerobic (or average) run but considerably easier than marathon race
pace. In this intensity range you find the optimal balance between running
hard enough to simulate the muscle patterns and posture you’ll use at
--- PAGE 238 ---
marathon race pace and running moderately enough that you can recover
relatively quickly for your other important training sessions.
Start out at the slow end of the range. Gradually pick up the pace so you
run the last 5 to 10 miles (8-16 km) at about 10 percent slower than your
goal marathon race pace. Gradually picking up the pace during your long
runs and finishing strongly will also provide positive psychological
reinforcement that you’re in control of the marathon. To gain the greatest
benefit, design your long-run courses to simulate the hill profile of your
marathon. (See the section on hill running later in this chapter.) If your
long-run course is much hillier than your marathon, your pace will be
somewhat slower.
The schedules also include marathon-specific long runs at goal marathon
race pace (discussed later in the chapter) and slower long runs the day after
a tune-up race. On the day after a race or hard workout, your long run
should be at a relaxed pace because you’re likely to be tired and stiff, which
will increase your likelihood of injury. Start these long runs like a recovery
run. If your muscles loosen up as the run progresses, increase the training
stimulus by increasing your pace to about 15 to 20 percent slower than
marathon race pace.
MEDIUM-LONG RUNS
A medium-long run is any run of 11 to 15 miles (18-24 km). Medium-long
runs reinforce the physiological benefits of your long runs. When your legs
are reasonably fresh, such as the day after a general aerobic run or recovery
run, the pace for these runs should be similar to the pace for long runs. If
you do a hard training session the day before a medium-long run, do the
medium-long run toward the slower end of the intensity range.
Avoid the temptation to do your medium-long runs too hard because this
will prolong your recovery time and reduce the quality of your other key
workouts. As with long runs, design your courses for medium-long runs to
simulate your marathon.
--- PAGE 239 ---
MARATHON-PACE RUNS
Marathon-pace runs are medium-long or long runs during which you run
most of the miles at your goal marathon pace. These runs provide the
precise physiological benefit of allowing you to practice the pace and form
of race day. They’re also a great confidence booster.
Start these runs comfortably, as you would other medium-long or long
runs, and then run the last portion at marathon race pace. For example, if
the schedule calls for 16 miles (26 km) with 12 miles (19 km) at marathon
race pace, gradually pick up the pace during the first 4 miles (6 km), and
then run the last 12 miles (19 km) at marathon goal pace. The objective of
these runs is to prepare your body as specifically as possible for your
upcoming marathon, so design your course to simulate your marathon as
closely as possible. For most well-trained marathoners, marathon pace
coincides with about 81 to 88 percent of maximal heart rate or 74 to 84
percent of heart rate reserve.
Matt Glynn
Fastest marathon: 2:30:43 (Twin Cities, 2004)
The most gratifying aspect of Advanced Marathoning’s success
is hearing from satisfied readers. That’s especially the case
when the readers are already dedicated, knowledgeable
runners who improved after following one of our schedules.
--- PAGE 240 ---
Description
Matt Glynn, of Buffalo, New York, is one such reader. A
member of his high school cross country team, Glynn ran on his
own in college and discovered how much he loves long-
distance running. He ran his first marathon at age 19, finishing
just under 3:30. He plugged away, aiming to break 3:00, which
he did in 1998 when he was in his late 20s. From there Glynn
continued to progress, all the way down to sub-2:40.
But Glynn knew he had more potential. “I was trying to break
2:30,” he says, “and needed a guide to get me there.” In 2002,
he started following the 12-week, 70-plus-miles-per week
schedule from the first edition of Advanced Marathoning. “I
made breakthroughs with these workouts,” Glynn says. “I’ve
now run 40 marathons, and my top nine times, all sub-2:40,
have come since I’ve used Advanced Marathoning plans.” That
includes his best of 2:30:43, set at Twin Cities in 2004, when
Glynn was in his mid-30s. Now in his mid-50s, Glynn still
--- PAGE 241 ---
follows a plan for his usual schedule of a spring and fall
marathon, topping out at 55 miles a week as a way to balance
proper preparation with the recognition that 80-mile weeks will
likely do him more harm than good.
“I appreciate the blend of speed workouts, medium-long runs,
and recovery runs, and how all of the science and logic behind
everything is spelled out in the book,” Glynn says. The biggest
changes to what he was doing on his own were the medium-
long runs and long runs with segments at marathon race pace.
“Those workouts were not easy, but I found the rest of the
week’s workouts balanced enough that I came into those
workouts still ready to go,” he says. “It took some adjusting, but
I understood the logic behind the workouts spelled out in the
book and stuck with it.”
On a practical level, his biggest adjustment was to the
midweek medium-long run. We’ve heard the same from many
readers. Most wind up doing like Glynn—namely, finding a way
to make it happen. That often means getting up however early
and having faith the extra work will pay off. “I’ve always strived
to carve out meaningful time for workouts at times when it won’t
take away from family time and work time, like early in the
morning,” Glynn, a reporter for the Buffalo News and father of
two boys, says. (Now that his sons are of college age, family
time is running time—they join Glynn for some workouts and
races.)
Elsewhere in this chapter we discuss readers’ tweaks to the
training schedules. Glynn has been more of a follow-it-as-best-
as-possible marathoner, except when hurt or sick. “I like
knowing I’m arriving at the starting line as prepared as I could
be, from following the plan meticulously,” he says. “I know each
workout has value in the bigger picture of fitness and
preparation. I trust in the training plan, and want to get
everything I can from it.”
Two decades past his personal best, Glynn has found other
ways to set meaningful time goals. “I try to base my goal times
off other races I run during the year and how things are going
--- PAGE 242 ---
with tune-up races before a marathon,” he says. “I find those
times give you a realistic picture of what you’re capable of.”
More subjectively, Glynn still aims to do his best in each
marathon. “Even if I’m not racing the pace I once did, the
distance—and often the course—pose enough of a challenge,”
he says. “There’s a satisfaction in knowing that I got the most
out of my training, even though my finish time goal isn’t
ambitious as it once was. I’ve struggled through enough
marathons to relish it on the days when a race goes right.
Those memories can carry you forward in future training runs
and future marathons.”
LACTATE-THRESHOLD RUNS
The lactate-threshold (LT) runs in the training schedules are tempo runs in
which you run for at least 20 minutes at your LT pace. The effort should be
the pace that you could race for about an hour. For most marathoners, this
pace range corresponds with about 83 to 90 percent of maximal heart rate or
77 to 87 percent of heart rate reserve. Another way to estimate LT pace for
experienced runners is about 10 to 15 seconds per mile slower than 10K
race pace. Tempo runs provide a strong stimulus to improve your LT pace,
which leads to similar improvements in your marathon race pace. The LT
sessions are done after a 2- to 3-mile (3-5 km) warm-up and should be
followed by about a 15-minute cool-down. The tempo runs in the schedules
range from 20 to 25 minutes up to 30 to 40 minutes for the 12-week
schedules and 35 to 45 minutes for the 18-week schedules. As an example,
if the schedule calls for 10 miles (16 km) for the day and 30 to 35 minutes
at LT pace, warm up for 3 miles (5 km), do the tempo run, then cool down
for 2 miles (3 km) or so to reach a total of 10 miles.
Slower runners obviously cover less distance in the same amount of
time, but because LT pace is the pace you could race for an hour, basing
these sessions on time is appropriate for faster and slower marathoners. In
previous editions of Advanced Marathoning, we prescribed the distance for
tempo runs and have responded to feedback from slower marathoners that,
--- PAGE 243 ---
for example, a 6-mile tempo run would take 50 minutes and it was a
struggle to maintain that effort for that long.
As mentioned in chapter 1, lactate threshold workouts can be done as
one continuous tempo run or as a long interval session at your LT pace
(termed LT intervals or cruise intervals). Table 8.1 provides examples of LT
interval workouts that are roughly equivalent alternatives to the tempo runs
in the training schedules. The recovery jog is intended to be brief enough
that you gain the full accumulated benefit of the two or three LT intervals in
each workout but long enough that you get an appropriate mental break
before the next effort. The jog between efforts starts gently, similar to the
jog between V O2max intervals, but as you feel ready, gradually pick up the
pace to standard recovery run effort.
Table 8.1 LT Interval Workout Alternatives
for Tempo Runs
Tempo run
duration LT interval workouts
20-25 min 3 × 8 min @ LT pace (3-min jog between)
12 min @ LT pace, jog 4 min, 10 min @ LT pace
10 min @ LT pace, jog 3 min, 8 min @ LT pace, jog 3 min, 6 min @
LT pace
25-30 min 3 × 9 min @ LT pace (3-min jog between)
15 min @ LT pace, jog 4 min, 12 min @ LT pace
12 min @ LT pace, jog 4 min, 10 min @ LT pace, jog 4 min, 8 min @
LT pace
30-35 min 3 × 11 min @ LT pace (3-min jog between)
18 min @ LT pace, jog 4 min, 14 min @ LT pace
14 min @ LT pace, jog 4 min, 11 min @ LT pace, jog 4 min, 8 min @
LT pace

20 min @ LT pace, jog 5 min, 16 min @ LT pace
16 min @ LT pace, jog 4 min, 12 min @ LT pace, jog 4 min, 8 min @
LT pace

22 min @ LT pace, jog 5 min, 20 min @ LT pace
18 min @ LT pace, jog 4 min, 14 min @ LT pace, jog 5 min, 10 min
@ LT pace
